<div id="review_52314524" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/52314524" />
    <a title="Evan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1248986-evan"><img alt="Evan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1488574589p2/1248986.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/52314524?book_show_action=true">Apr 11, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Evan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Evan" href="/user/show/1248986-evan">Evan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780553279306" href="/book/show/887780.Being_There">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=__in-my-collection">__in-my-collection</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=politics">politics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=2009-reads">2009-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=humor">humor</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer52314524" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6325690710417907523">So, there was a German film in the mid-1970s, a Werner Herzog-directed movie of cult status titled, The Mystery of Kaspar Hauser, based supposedly on a true incident from hundreds of years ago in which a man, a kind of idiot, suddenly appeared in a town and his origins remained a mystery. In the film, Hauser is shown to be raised in a dark, prison-like condition, cut off completely from the ways of the world and socialization. Raised like some factory farm animal. Then one day his caretaker dies</span>
  <span id="freeText6325690710417907523" style="display:none">So, there was a German film in the mid-1970s, a Werner Herzog-directed movie of cult status titled, The Mystery of Kaspar Hauser, based supposedly on a true incident from hundreds of years ago in which a man, a kind of idiot, suddenly appeared in a town and his origins remained a mystery. In the film, Hauser is shown to be raised in a dark, prison-like condition, cut off completely from the ways of the world and socialization. Raised like some factory farm animal. Then one day his caretaker dies and he is released to the world, entirely ignorant. Goes into a village where his mysterious presence asserts a strange magic over the townsfolk all the way up to the leadership, including the mayor. Things are attributed to him that have no bearing in reality. People chose to see him as they will, to suit their own ends. But such enigma can attract power and cult and threaten the powerful and status quo. Hauser comes to be regarded as a nuisance to be gotten rid of...<br />There are all kinds of parallel parables to that story: The story of Christ being one, but a lot of satires (screwball movie comedies of the '30s and old stage farces up to today's movies) have rehashed this idea: the dumb guy thrust into society and taken for something more than he is.<br />So the idea for Kosinski's famous novel is not exactly original.<br /><br />The good news is: it's a delightful little book, a clever story. Simple, fast-moving, not always entirely convincing, but mostly on-target.<br /><br />The 1979 film adaptation with Peter Sellers certainly equals it, and maybe even surpasses it. It has to be one of the best and most faithful movie adaptations of a novel ever.<br /><br />So I laughed several times at the ways in which the power elites misinterpret Chauncey Gardiner's simplistic, straightforward accounts of gardening as metaphors for the vagaries of business and politics.<br /><br />The story is fairly well known: an anonymous nobody raised inside the walls of a house and garden, who knows nothing but gardening and whose socialization comes entirely from lifelong TV watching, is suddenly thrust into the world and through a mishap and a few well-timed but misinterpreted quotes becomes a confidante of the powerful, and a media sensation. People read into him their own agendas and desires. And, as he says, he just likes to watch. Watches it all happen as they write on his blank slate.<br /><br />It's not a great novel, but as I say it's a fun story, done with dispatch.<br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6325690710417907523" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1856022068" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1856022068" />
    <a title="Thom" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2945070-thom"><img alt="Thom" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1422135250p2/2945070.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1856022068?book_show_action=true">Jan 03, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Thom" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Thom" href="/user/show/2945070-thom">Thom</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/6620932-being-there">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2945070-thom?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1856022068" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10707082149662744165">This review focuses solely on the book, as it's been decades since I've seen the film. Chance the gardner is used to prove the point that the clothes make the man. The first impression is of a neatly tailored shirt and suit, excellent shoes, a briefcase that is in excellent shape if quite a few years old. (The suit fits, of course, because Chance is likely the bastard son of their previous owner). Considering that impression and the location (the home of a wealthy man, a UN conference, etc.), pe</span>
  <span id="freeText10707082149662744165" style="display:none">This review focuses solely on the book, as it's been decades since I've seen the film. Chance the gardner is used to prove the point that the clothes make the man. The first impression is of a neatly tailored shirt and suit, excellent shoes, a briefcase that is in excellent shape if quite a few years old. (The suit fits, of course, because Chance is likely the bastard son of their previous owner). Considering that impression and the location (the home of a wealthy man, a UN conference, etc.), people fit his simple replies into their own projections, hearing mostly what they want to hear in the echoing vacuum. Later in the book, governments trying to find out more about him are convinced their must be more because other governments are seeking diligently themselves.<br /><br />This commentary of people is accurate. Other parts of the book seem tacked on, e.g. Chance's seduction. The novel ends quickly with no solid resolution - I recall the movie improved on this, but will watch it again to find out. For me this was an audiobook, narrated by Dustin Hoffman, who does an excellent job. No verdict yet on this author, though I plan to tackle his most famous work, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18452.The_Painted_Bird" title="The Painted Bird by Jerzy Kosi≈Ñski" rel="nofollow noopener">The Painted Bird</a>, soon.</span>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2746616754?book_show_action=true">Nov 16, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cheri" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cheri" href="/user/show/106692-cheri">Cheri</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=audible">audible</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=humor">humor</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2746616754" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15226731612613216919"><br/>Chance has been the gardener of a home owned by an elderly man for as long as he can remember. Chance, a simple, illiterate man, has lived in this house his whole life, when the man passes away. No provisions in his will, nothing among his paperwork even references Chance. There are no records, seemingly anywhere that share his story, or even mention him as being part of the household. <br/><br/>Chance has spent little time outside in the world, but he does know how it is portrayed through many hours of w</span>
  <span id="freeText15226731612613216919" style="display:none"><br />Chance has been the gardener of a home owned by an elderly man for as long as he can remember. Chance, a simple, illiterate man, has lived in this house his whole life, when the man passes away. No provisions in his will, nothing among his paperwork even references Chance. There are no records, seemingly anywhere that share his story, or even mention him as being part of the household. <br /><br />Chance has spent little time outside in the world, but he does know how it is portrayed through many hours of watching television. Armed with this knowledge and some second-hand, but fine clothes that had belonged to the deceased, he sets out into the world only to be hit by a limousine. Both the chauffeur and passenger, Mrs. Rand, feeling very concerned over his welfare - or perhaps being sued, insists he return with her to her home, where she will have her husband‚Äôs doctor attend to his injuries. When asked for his name, his response is misunderstood, and thereafter he is referred to by the Rands, their doctor, and eventually the world, as Chauncey Gardiner. The Rands, as a couple who are friends with many of the rich and powerful, are impressed by the way that he has of taking complicated issues and distilling his view on them to the simplest form, which all from the Rands to the US President, a friend of the Rands, consider to be profound. When asked to speak on complex political, and other, topics, his responses refer to his experience gardening, literally, but others interpret them as profoundly brilliant analogies. <br /> <br />I‚Äôd seen the movie years ago, and had forgotten much of the story, but remembered being charmed by it, so was happy to ‚Äòread‚Äô the story courtesy or Audible. This version is narrated by Dustin Hoffman, which made it even more of a joy to listen to, and oddly fitting for our time. <br /></span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/226085736" />
    <a title="Erin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3413113-erin"><img alt="Erin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1409063300p2/3413113.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/226085736?book_show_action=true">Oct 21, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Erin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Erin" href="/user/show/3413113-erin">Erin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer226085736" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4098347799819775736">At only 140 pages, this was a very quick read but fun.<br/><br/>Chance was sheltered from the outside world his entire life. He never left the house of his employer except to tend the garden in the backyard. He cannot read or write and spends his off time watching TV. When his employer dies he is left homeless and thrust into the outside world. After an accident and a few gardening metaphors he is thrust into America's financial and political society.<br/><br/>I found this book incredibly humorous. I loved the iron</span>
  <span id="freeText4098347799819775736" style="display:none">At only 140 pages, this was a very quick read but fun.<br /><br />Chance was sheltered from the outside world his entire life. He never left the house of his employer except to tend the garden in the backyard. He cannot read or write and spends his off time watching TV. When his employer dies he is left homeless and thrust into the outside world. After an accident and a few gardening metaphors he is thrust into America's financial and political society.<br /><br />I found this book incredibly humorous. I loved the irony of the entire situation. How someone who knows nothing and says very little can rise to fame all because of assumptions and media perception.<br /><br />Kosinski's writing is simple and to the point which made it very easy to read. The subject matter is relevant probably more today than it was when it was written in 1970. I think it's nearly impossible to read this book and not insert a politician from your life. It makes you think about politics and media.<br /><br />Being There would be a great book for a book club. So many discussions could come out of this book.<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://theultimatebooknook.blogspot.com/2011/10/being-there-by-jerzy-kosinski.html">http://theultimatebooknook.blogspot.c...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="4098347799819775736" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('ZOI5QvZbLZZhD4XNvRusJ1LRvO927sUTSDhF9BSDbxFfj+CwZ2fGdVv02HNz7++Vb/+GELsu6/BYTWrP3yDqrA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('AteJfJNgXGFEqndCeUM5uX+nm1S7DZSZt4hsDW/zPPo5ulCOAly3gn5RKvy3t3oLQomhq3bNunqn/UM2pFC5Rw==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

The difficulty that he found in providing these Appendices, leading to the delay in the publication of The Return of the King, is well known, but in The Peoples of Middle-earth Christopher Tolkien shows that early forms of these works already existed years before, in essays and records differing greatly from the published forms. He traces the evolution of the Calendars, the Hobbit genealogies, the Westron language or Common Speach (from which many words and names are recorded that were afterwards lost), and the chronological structure of the later Ages.\n\nOther writings by J.R.R. Tolkien are included in this final volume of The History of MIddle-earth, chiefly deriving from his last years, when new insights and new constructions still freely arose as he pondered the history that he had created.             var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });
      });

//]]>
</script>

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/11121.Jerzy_Kosi_ski">About Jerzy Kosi≈Ñski</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/11121.Jerzy_Kosi_ski"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1342908489p3/11121.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/11121.Jerzy_Kosi_ski">Jerzy Kosi≈Ñski
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
465
followers
</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer10841549584723503393">Kosi≈Ñski was born Josef Lewinkopf to Jewish parents in ≈Å√≥d≈∫, Poland. As a child during World War II, he lived in central Poland under a false identity his father gave him to use, Jerzy Kosi≈Ñski. A Roman Catholic priest issued him a forged baptismal certificate. The Kosi≈Ñski family survived the Holocaust thanks to local villagers, who offered assistance to Jewish Poles often at great personal risk </span>
  <span id="freeText10841549584723503393" style="display:none">Kosi≈Ñski was born Josef Lewinkopf to Jewish parents in ≈Å√≥d≈∫, Poland. As a child during World War II, he lived in central Poland under a false identity his father gave him to use, Jerzy Kosi≈Ñski. A Roman Catholic priest issued him a forged baptismal certificate. The Kosi≈Ñski family survived the Holocaust thanks to local villagers, who offered assistance to Jewish Poles often at great personal risk (the penalty for assisting Jews in Nazi-occupied Poland was death). Kosi≈Ñski's father received help not only from Polish town leaders and churchmen, but also from individuals such as Marianna Pasiowa, a member of the Polish underground network helping Jews to evade capture. The family lived openly in DƒÖbrowa Rzeczycka near Stalowa Wola, and attended church in nearby Wola Rzeczycka, obtaining support from villagers in Kƒôpa Rzeczycka. They were sheltered temporarily by a Catholic family in Rzeczyca OkrƒÖg≈Ça. The young Jerzy even served as an altar boy in a local church.<br /><br />After World War II, Kosi≈Ñski remained with his parents in Poland, moved to Jelenia G√≥ra, and earned degrees in history and political science at the University of ≈Å√≥d≈∫. He worked as an assistant in Institute of History and Sociology at the Polish Academy of Sciences. In 1957, he emigrated to the United States, creating a fake foundation which supposedly sponsored him; he later claimed that the letters from eminent Polish communist authorities guaranteeing his loyal return, which were needed for anyone leaving the communist country at that time, had all been forged by him.<br /><br />After taking odd jobs to get by, such as driving a truck, Kosi≈Ñski graduated from Columbia University. In 1965, he became an American citizen. He received grants from Guggenheim Fellowship in 1967, Ford Foundation in 1968, and the American Academy in 1970, which allowed him to write a political non-fiction book, opening new doors of opportunity. In the States he became a lecturer at Yale, Princeton, Davenport University, and Wesleyan.<br /><br />In 1962 Kosi≈Ñski married Mary Hayward Weir who was 10 years his senior. They were divorced in 1966. Weir died in 1968 from brain cancer. Kosi≈Ñski was left nothing in her will. He later fictionalized this marriage in his novel Blind Date speaking of Weir under pseudonym Mary-Jane Kirkland. Kosi≈Ñski went on to marry Katherina "Kiki" von Fraunhofer, a marketing consultant and descendant of Bavarian aristocracy. They met in 1968.<br /><br /><b>Death</b><br /><br />Kosi≈Ñski suffered from multiple illnesses towards the end of his life, and was under attack from journalists who alleged he was a plagiarist. By the time he reached his late 50s, Kosi≈Ñski was suffering from an irregular heartbeat as well as severe physical and nervous exhaustion. Kosi≈Ñski committed suicide on May 3, 1991, by taking a fatal dose of barbiturates. His parting suicide note read: "I am going to put myself to sleep now for a bit longer than usual. Call it Eternity".</span>
  <a data-text-id="10841549584723503393" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
